class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><td style="padding-top:0.4em;line-height:1.2em;background:#cdc8b8">Part of <a href="/wiki/Category:Ancient_Near_East_mythology" title="Category:Ancient Near East mythology">a series</a> on</td></tr><tr><th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;padding-top:0;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;background:#cdc8b8; font-size:120%"><a href="/wiki/Religions_of_the_ancient_Near_East" title="Religions of the ancient Near East">Religions of the ancient Near East</a></th></tr><tr><td class="hlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_mythology" title="Hittite mythology">Anatolia</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Ancient Egypt</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Religion_in_pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Religion in pre-Islamic Arabia">Arabia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Canaanite_religion" title="Ancient Canaanite religion">Canaan</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Persian_mythology" title="Persian mythology">Persia</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Mesopotamian_religion" title="Ancient Mesopotamian religion">Semitic</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Ancient_Near_East_mythology" title="Template:Ancient Near East mythology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Ancient_Near_East_mythology" title="Template talk:Ancient Near East mythology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Ancient_Near_East_mythology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Ancient Egyptian religion</b> was a complex system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytheistic" title="Polytheistic">polytheistic</a> beliefs and rituals that formed an integral part of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egyptian</a> society.  It centered on the Egyptians\' interaction with many <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deities</a> believed to be present in, and in control of, the world. Rituals such as prayer and offerings were provided to the gods to gain their favor. Formal religious practice centered on the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a>, the rulers of Egypt, believed to possess a divine power by virtue of their position. They acted as intermediaries between their people and the gods, and were obligated to sustain the gods through rituals and offerings so that they could maintain <i><a href="/wiki/Maat" title="Maat">maat</a></i>, the order of the cosmos. The state dedicated enormous resources to Egyptian rituals and to the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_temple" title="Egyptian temple">temples</a>.\n</p><p>Individuals could interact with the gods for their own purposes, appealing for help through prayer or compelling the gods to act through magic. These practices were distinct from, but closely linked with, the formal rituals and institutions. The popular religious tradition grew more prominent in the course of Egyptian history as the status of the pharaoh declined. Egyptian belief in the afterlife and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_burial_customs" title="Ancient Egyptian burial customs">funerary practices</a> is evident in great efforts made to ensure the survival of their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_soul" title="Egyptian soul">souls</a> after death, providing tombs, grave goods, and offerings to preserve the bodies and spirits of the deceased.\n</p><p>The religion had its roots in Egypt\'s prehistory and lasted for more than 3,000 years. The details of religious belief changed over time as the importance of particular gods rose and declined, and their intricate relationships shifted.  At various times, certain gods became preeminent over the others, including the sun god <a href="/wiki/Ra" title="Ra">Ra</a>, the creator god <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a>, and the mother goddess <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a>. For a brief period, in the <a href="/wiki/Atenism" title="Atenism">theology</a> promulgated by the Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Akhenaten" title="Akhenaten">Akhenaten</a>, a single god, the <a href="/wiki/Aten" title="Aten">Aten</a>, replaced the traditional pantheon. Ancient Egyptian religion and mythology left behind many writings and monuments, along with significant influences on ancient and modern cultures.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs">Beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The beliefs and rituals now referred to as "ancient Egyptian religion" were integral within every aspect of Egyptian culture. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_language" title="Ancient Egyptian language">The Egyptian language</a> possessed no single term corresponding to the modern European concept of religion. Ancient Egyptian religion consisted of a vast and varying set of beliefs and practices, linked by their common focus on the interaction between the world of humans and the world of the divine. The characteristics of the gods who populated the divine realm were inextricably linked to the Egyptians\' understanding of the properties of the world in which they lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann20011&#8211;5,_80_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann20011&#8211;5,_80-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deities">Deities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Deities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_deities" title="Ancient Egyptian deities">Ancient Egyptian deities</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_Tombe_de_Horemheb_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="514" data-file-width="372" decoding="async" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/La_Tombe_de_Horemheb_cropped.jpg/220px-La_Tombe_de_Horemheb_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/La_Tombe_de_Horemheb_cropped.jpg/330px-La_Tombe_de_Horemheb_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/La_Tombe_de_Horemheb_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_Tombe_de_Horemheb_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The gods Osiris, Anubis, and Horus, in order from left to right</div></div></div>\n<p>The Egyptians believed that the phenomena of nature were divine forces in and of themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann200163&#8211;64,_82_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann200163&#8211;64,_82-2">[2]</a></sup>  These deified forces included the elements, animal characteristics, or abstract forces.  The Egyptians believed in a pantheon of gods, which were involved in all aspects of nature and human society. Their religious practices were efforts to sustain and placate these phenomena and turn them to human advantage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_43_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen_43-3">[3]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheistic</a> system was very complex, as some deities were believed to exist in many different manifestations, and some had multiple mythological roles.  Conversely, many natural forces, such as the sun, were associated with multiple deities.  The diverse pantheon ranged from gods with vital roles in the universe to minor deities or "demons" with very limited or localized functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200330,_32,_89_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200330,_32,_89-4">[4]</a></sup>  It could include gods adopted from foreign cultures, and sometimes humans: deceased pharaohs were believed to be divine, and occasionally, distinguished commoners such as <a href="/wiki/Imhotep" title="Imhotep">Imhotep</a> also became deified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESilverman199155&#8211;58_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESilverman199155&#8211;58-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The depictions of the gods in <a href="/wiki/Art_of_ancient_Egypt" title="Art of ancient Egypt">art</a> were not meant as literal representations of how the gods might appear if they were visible, as the gods\' true natures were believed to be mysterious. Instead, these depictions gave recognizable forms to the abstract deities by using symbolic imagery to indicate each god\'s role in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid200253_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid200253-6">[6]</a></sup>   This iconography was not fixed, and many of the gods could be depicted in more than one form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200328_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200328-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many gods were associated with particular regions in Egypt where their cults were most important. However, these associations changed over time, and they did not mean that the god associated with a place had originated there. For instance, the god <a href="/wiki/Montu" title="Montu">Montu</a> was the original patron of the city of Thebes. Over the course of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom</a>, however, he was displaced in that role by Amun, who may have arisen elsewhere. The national popularity and importance of individual gods fluctuated in a similar way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeeter2001340&#8211;44_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeeter2001340&#8211;44-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Deities had complex interrelationships, which partly reflected the interaction of the forces they represented. The Egyptians often grouped gods together to reflect these relationships. One of the more common combinations was a family triad consisting of a father, mother, and child, who were worshipped together. Some groups had wide-ranging importance. One such group, the <a href="/wiki/Ennead" title="Ennead">Ennead</a>, assembled nine deities into a theological system that was involved in the mythological areas of creation, kingship, and the afterlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200374&#8211;79_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200374&#8211;79-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The relationships between deities could also be expressed in the process of <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a>, in which two or more different gods were linked to form a composite deity.  This process was a recognition of the presence of one god "in" another when the second god took on a role belonging to the first.  These links between deities were fluid, and did not represent the permanent merging of two gods into one; therefore, some gods could develop multiple syncretic connections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200527&#8211;28_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200527&#8211;28-10">[10]</a></sup> Sometimes, syncretism combined deities with very similar characteristics. At other times it joined gods with very different natures, as when Amun, the god of hidden power, was linked with <a href="/wiki/Ra" title="Ra">Ra</a>, the god of the sun. The resulting god, Amun-Ra, thus united the power that lay behind all things with the greatest and most visible force in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200333&#8211;35_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200333&#8211;35-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many deities could be given epithets that seem to indicate that they were greater than any other god, suggesting some kind of unity beyond the multitude of natural forces. This is particularly true of a few gods who, at various points, rose to supreme importance in Egyptian religion. These included the royal patron <a href="/wiki/Horus" title="Horus">Horus</a>, the sun god <a href="/wiki/Ra" title="Ra">Ra</a>, and the mother goddess <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200336,_67_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200336,_67-12">[12]</a></sup>  During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Kingdom" title="New Kingdom">New Kingdom</a> (c. 1550&#8211;1070 BC) Amun held this position. The theology of the period described in particular detail Amun\'s presence in and rule over all things, so that he, more than any other deity, embodied the all-encompassing power of the divine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann2001189&#8211;92,_241&#8211;42_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann2001189&#8211;92,_241&#8211;42-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmology">Cosmology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cosmology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Geb,_Nut,_Shu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1140" data-file-width="1691" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Geb%2C_Nut%2C_Shu.jpg/280px-Geb%2C_Nut%2C_Shu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Geb%2C_Nut%2C_Shu.jpg/420px-Geb%2C_Nut%2C_Shu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Geb%2C_Nut%2C_Shu.jpg/560px-Geb%2C_Nut%2C_Shu.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Geb,_Nut,_Shu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The air god Shu, assisted by other gods, holds up Nut, the sky, as Geb, the earth, lies beneath.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Egyptian conception of the universe centered on <i><a href="/wiki/Maat" title="Maat">maat</a></i>, a word that encompasses several concepts in English, including "truth", "justice", and "order."  It was the fixed, eternal order of the universe, both in the cosmos and in human society, and it was often personified as a goddess. It had existed since the creation of the world, and without it the world would lose its cohesion. In Egyptian belief, <i>maat</i> was constantly under threat from the forces of disorder, so all of society was required to maintain it. On the human level this meant that all members of society should cooperate and coexist; on the cosmic level it meant that all of the forces of nature&#8212;the gods&#8212;should continue to function in balance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_115_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen_115-14">[14]</a></sup> This latter goal was central to Egyptian religion. The Egyptians sought to maintain <i>maat</i> in the cosmos by sustaining the gods through offerings and by performing rituals which staved off disorder and perpetuated the cycles of nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann20014&#8211;5_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann20014&#8211;5-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shafer_2_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shafer_2-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most important part of the Egyptian view of the cosmos was the conception of time, which was greatly concerned with the maintenance of Maat. Throughout the linear passage of time, a cyclical pattern recurred, in which Maat was renewed by periodic events which echoed the original creation. Among these events were the annual Nile flood and the succession from one king to another, but the most important was the daily journey of the sun god Ra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann200168&#8211;79_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann200168&#8211;79-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen2000104,_127_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen2000104,_127-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When thinking of the shape of the cosmos, the Egyptians saw the earth as a flat expanse of land, personified by the god <a href="/wiki/Geb" title="Geb">Geb</a>, over which arched the sky goddess <a href="/wiki/Nut_(goddess)" title="Nut (goddess)">Nut</a>. The two were separated by Shu, the god of air. Beneath the earth lay a parallel <a href="/wiki/Underworld" title="Underworld">underworld</a> and undersky, and beyond the skies lay the infinite expanse of <a href="/wiki/Nu_(mythology)" title="Nu (mythology)">Nu</a>, the chaos that had existed before creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELesko1991117&#8211;21_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELesko1991117&#8211;21-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200545&#8211;46_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200545&#8211;46-20">[20]</a></sup>  The Egyptians also believed in a place called the <a href="/wiki/Duat" title="Duat">Duat</a>, a mysterious region associated with death and rebirth, that may have lain in the underworld or in the sky.  Each day, Ra traveled over the earth across the underside of the sky, and at night he passed through the Duat to be reborn at dawn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Egyptian belief, this cosmos was inhabited by three types of sentient beings. One was the gods; another was the spirits of deceased humans, who existed in the divine realm and possessed many of the gods\' abilities. Living humans were the third category, and the most important among them was the pharaoh, who bridged the human and divine realms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen200031_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen200031-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%84gyptisches_Museum_Kairo_2016-03-29_Chephren_03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3270" data-file-width="4905" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/%C3%84gyptisches_Museum_Kairo_2016-03-29_Chephren_03.jpg/280px-%C3%84gyptisches_Museum_Kairo_2016-03-29_Chephren_03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/%C3%84gyptisches_Museum_Kairo_2016-03-29_Chephren_03.jpg/420px-%C3%84gyptisches_Museum_Kairo_2016-03-29_Chephren_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/%C3%84gyptisches_Museum_Kairo_2016-03-29_Chephren_03.jpg/560px-%C3%84gyptisches_Museum_Kairo_2016-03-29_Chephren_03.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C3%84gyptisches_Museum_Kairo_2016-03-29_Chephren_03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khafre" title="Khafre">Khafre</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Old Kingdom of Egypt">Old Kingdom</a> pharaoh, embraced by <a href="/wiki/Horus" title="Horus">Horus</a></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingship">Kingship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Kingship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaoh</a></div>\n<p>Egyptologists have long debated the degree to which the pharaoh was considered a god. It seems most likely that the Egyptians viewed royal authority itself as a divine force.  Therefore, although the Egyptians recognized that the pharaoh was human and subject to human weakness, they simultaneously viewed him as a god, because the divine power of kingship was incarnated in him. He therefore acted as intermediary between Egypt\'s people and the gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200354&#8211;56_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200354&#8211;56-23">[23]</a></sup>  He was key to upholding Maat, both by maintaining justice and harmony in human society and by sustaining the gods with temples and offerings. For these reasons, he oversaw all state religious activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann20015&#8211;6_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann20015&#8211;6-24">[24]</a></sup>  However, the pharaoh\'s real-life influence and prestige could differ from his portrayal in official writings and depictions, and beginning in the late New Kingdom his religious importance declined drastically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200355_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200355-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The king was also associated with many specific deities. He was identified directly with Horus, who represented kingship itself, and he was seen as the son of Ra, who ruled and regulated nature as the pharaoh ruled and regulated society.  By the New Kingdom he was also associated with Amun, the supreme force in the cosmos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid200269,_95,_184_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid200269,_95,_184-27">[27]</a></sup>  Upon his death, the king became fully deified. In this state, he was directly identified with Ra, and was also associated with <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osiris</a>, god of death and rebirth and the mythological father of Horus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200360&#8211;63_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200360&#8211;63-28">[28]</a></sup>  Many mortuary temples were dedicated to the worship of deceased pharaohs as gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shafer_2_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shafer_2-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Afterlife">Afterlife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Afterlife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_afterlife_beliefs" title="Ancient Egyptian afterlife beliefs">Ancient Egyptian afterlife beliefs</a></div>\n<p>The Egyptians had elaborate beliefs about death and the afterlife. They believed that humans possessed a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_soul#Ka" title="Egyptian soul">ka</a></i>, or life-force, which left the body at the point of death. In life, the <i>ka</i> received its sustenance from food and drink, so it was believed that, to endure after death, the <i>ka</i> must continue to receive offerings of food, whose spiritual essence it could still consume. Each person also had a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_soul#Ba" title="Egyptian soul">ba</a></i>, the set of spiritual characteristics unique to each individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen200079&#8211;80_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen200079&#8211;80-29">[29]</a></sup>  Unlike the <i>ka</i>, the <i>ba</i> remained attached to the body after death. Egyptian funeral rituals were intended to release the <i>ba</i> from the body so that it could move freely, and to rejoin it with the <i>ka</i> so that it could live on as an <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_soul#Akh" title="Egyptian soul">akh</a></i>. However, it was also important that the body of the deceased be preserved, as the Egyptians believed that the <i>ba</i> returned to its body each night to receive new life, before emerging in the morning as an <i>akh</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen200094&#8211;95_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen200094&#8211;95-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In early times the deceased pharaoh was believed to <a href="/wiki/The_Indestructibles#Cosmogony" title="The Indestructibles">ascend to the sky and dwell among the stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor200125_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor200125-31">[31]</a></sup>  Over the course of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom" title="Old Kingdom">Old Kingdom</a> (c. 2686&#8211;2181 BC), however, he came to be more closely associated with the daily rebirth of the sun god <a href="/wiki/Ra" title="Ra">Ra</a> and with the underworld ruler <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osiris</a> as those deities grew more important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid200290,_94&#8211;95_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid200290,_94&#8211;95-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the fully developed afterlife beliefs of the New Kingdom, the soul had to avoid a variety of supernatural dangers in the <a href="/wiki/Duat" title="Duat">Duat</a>, before undergoing a final judgment, known as the "Weighing of the Heart", carried out by Osiris and by the <a href="/wiki/Assessors_of_Maat" title="Assessors of Maat">Assessors of Maat</a>. In this judgment, the gods compared the actions of the deceased while alive (symbolized by the heart) to Maat, to determine whether he or she had behaved in accordance with Maat. If the deceased was judged worthy, his or her <i>ka</i> and <i>ba</i> were united into an <i>akh</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian1997104_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian1997104-33">[33]</a></sup>  Several beliefs coexisted about the <i>akh</i>\'s destination. Often the dead were said to dwell in the realm of Osiris, a lush and pleasant land in the <a href="/wiki/Underworld" title="Underworld">underworld</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002160&#8211;61_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002160&#8211;61-34">[34]</a></sup> The solar vision of the afterlife, in which the deceased soul traveled with Ra on his daily journey, was still primarily associated with royalty, but could extend to other people as well. Over the course of the Middle and New Kingdoms, the notion that the <i>akh</i> could also travel in the world of the living, and to some degree magically affect events there, became increasingly prevalent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann2005209&#8211;10,_398&#8211;402_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann2005209&#8211;10,_398&#8211;402-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atenism">Atenism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Atenism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atenism" title="Atenism">Atenism</a></div>\n<p>During the New Kingdom the pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Akhenaten" title="Akhenaten">Akhenaten</a> abolished the official worship of other gods in favor of the sun-disk <a href="/wiki/Aten" title="Aten">Aten</a>. This is often seen as the first instance of true monotheism in history, although the details of Atenist theology are still unclear and the suggestion that it was monotheistic is disputed. The exclusion of all but one god from worship was a radical departure from Egyptian tradition and some see Akhenaten as a practitioner of <a href="/wiki/Monolatry" title="Monolatry">monolatry</a> rather than monotheism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> as he did not actively deny the existence of other gods; he simply refrained from worshipping any but the Aten. Under Akhenaten\'s successors Egypt reverted to its traditional religion, and Akhenaten himself came to be reviled as a heretic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200535_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200535-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen2000198_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen2000198-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings">Writings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Writings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_literature" title="Ancient Egyptian literature">Ancient Egyptian literature</a></div>\n<p>While the Egyptians had no unified religious scripture, they produced many religious writings of various types. Together the disparate texts provide an extensive, but still incomplete, understanding of Egyptian religious practices and beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETraunecker20011&#8211;5_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETraunecker20011&#8211;5-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology">Mythology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mythology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_mythology" title="Egyptian mythology">Egyptian mythology</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Book_of_Gates_Barque_of_Ra_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="215" data-file-width="355" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Book_of_Gates_Barque_of_Ra_cropped.jpg/300px-Book_of_Gates_Barque_of_Ra_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/19/Book_of_Gates_Barque_of_Ra_cropped.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Book_of_Gates_Barque_of_Ra_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ra" title="Ra">Ra</a> (at center) travels through the underworld in his barque, accompanied by other gods<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson2003222&#8211;223_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson2003222&#8211;223-41">[41]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Egyptian myths were metaphorical stories intended to illustrate and explain the gods\' actions and roles in nature. The details of the events they recounted could change to convey different symbolic perspectives on the mysterious divine events they described, so many myths exist in different and conflicting versions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETobin2001464&#8211;68_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETobin2001464&#8211;68-42">[42]</a></sup>  Mythical narratives were rarely written in full, and more often texts only contain episodes from or allusions to a larger myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinch199518_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinch199518-43">[43]</a></sup>  Knowledge of Egyptian mythology, therefore, is derived mostly from hymns that detail the roles of specific deities, from ritual and magical texts which describe actions related to mythic events, and from funerary texts which mention the roles of many deities in the afterlife. Some information is also provided by allusions in secular texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETraunecker20011&#8211;5_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETraunecker20011&#8211;5-40">[40]</a></sup>  Finally, Greeks and Romans such as <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> recorded some of the extant myths late in Egyptian history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian199726_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian199726-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Among the significant Egyptian myths were the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_creation_myths" title="Ancient Egyptian creation myths">creation myths</a>. According to these stories, the world emerged as a dry space in the primordial ocean of chaos.  Because the sun is essential to life on earth, the first rising of Ra marked the moment of this emergence. Different forms of the myth describe the process of creation in various ways: a transformation of the primordial god <a href="/wiki/Atum" title="Atum">Atum</a> into the elements that form the world, as the creative speech of the intellectual god <a href="/wiki/Ptah" title="Ptah">Ptah</a>, and as an act of the hidden power of Amun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen2000143&#8211;45,_171&#8211;73,_182_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen2000143&#8211;45,_171&#8211;73,_182-45">[45]</a></sup>  Regardless of these variations, the act of creation represented the initial establishment of maat and the pattern for the subsequent cycles of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shafer_2_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Shafer_2-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most important of all Egyptian myths was the <a href="/wiki/Osiris_myth" title="Osiris myth">Osiris myth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann2001124_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann2001124-46">[46]</a></sup>  It tells of the divine ruler Osiris, who was murdered by his jealous brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Set_(mythology)" title="Set (mythology)">Set</a>, a god often associated with chaos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian199776,_78_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian199776,_78-47">[47]</a></sup>  Osiris\'s sister and wife <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a> resurrected him so that he could conceive an heir, Horus.  Osiris then entered the underworld and became the ruler of the dead.  Once grown, Horus fought and defeated Set to become king himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuirkeSpencer199267_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuirkeSpencer199267-48">[48]</a></sup>  Set\'s association with chaos, and the identification of Osiris and Horus as the rightful rulers, provided a rationale for Pharaonic succession and portrayed the Pharaohs as the upholders of order.  At the same time, Osiris\'s death and rebirth were related to the Egyptian agricultural cycle, in which crops grew in the wake of the Nile inundation, and provided a template for the resurrection of human souls after death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian199784,_107&#8211;108_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian199784,_107&#8211;108-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another important mythic motif was the journey of Ra through the Duat each night. In the course of this journey, Ra met with Osiris, who again acted as an agent of regeneration, so that his life was renewed. He also fought each night with <a href="/wiki/Apep" title="Apep">Apep</a>, a serpentine god representing chaos. The defeat of Apep and the meeting with Osiris ensured the rising of the sun the next morning, an event that represented rebirth and the victory of order over chaos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian199733,_38&#8211;39_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian199733,_38&#8211;39-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ritual_and_magical_texts">Ritual and magical texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ritual and magical texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The procedures for religious rituals were frequently written on papyri, which were used as instructions for those performing the ritual.  These ritual texts were kept mainly in the temple libraries. Temples themselves are also inscribed with such texts, often accompanied by illustrations. Unlike the ritual papyri, these inscriptions were not intended as instructions, but were meant to symbolically perpetuate the rituals even if, in reality, people ceased to perform them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200593&#8211;99_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200593&#8211;99-51">[51]</a></sup>  Magical texts likewise describe rituals, although these rituals were part of the spells used for specific goals in everyday life. Despite their mundane purpose, many of these texts also originated in temple libraries and later became disseminated among the general populace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinch199563_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinch199563-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hymns_and_prayers">Hymns and prayers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Hymns and prayers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Egyptians produced numerous prayers and hymns, written in the form of poetry. Hymns and prayers follow a similar structure and are distinguished mainly by the purposes they serve. Hymns were written to praise particular deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foster_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foster-53">[53]</a></sup> Like ritual texts, they were written on papyri and on temple walls, and they were probably recited as part of the rituals they accompany in temple inscriptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200594_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200594-54">[54]</a></sup>  Most are structured according to a set literary formula, designed to expound on the nature, aspects, and mythological functions of a given deity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foster_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foster-53">[53]</a></sup> They tend to speak more explicitly about fundamental theology than other Egyptian religious writings, and became particularly important in the New Kingdom, a period of particularly active theological discourse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann2001166_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann2001166-55">[55]</a></sup>  Prayers follow the same general pattern as hymns, but address the relevant god in a more personal way, asking for blessings, help, or forgiveness for wrongdoing. Such prayers are rare before the New Kingdom, indicating that in earlier periods such direct personal interaction with a deity was not believed possible, or at least was less likely to be expressed in writing. They are known mainly from inscriptions on statues and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stelae" title="Stelae">stelae</a> left in sacred sites as <a href="/wiki/Votive_offering" title="Votive offering">votive offerings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ockinga_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ockinga-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funerary_texts">Funerary texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Funerary texts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_funerary_texts" title="Ancient Egyptian funerary texts">Ancient Egyptian funerary texts</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BD_Hunefer_cropped_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="493" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/BD_Hunefer_cropped_1.jpg/250px-BD_Hunefer_cropped_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/BD_Hunefer_cropped_1.jpg/375px-BD_Hunefer_cropped_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/BD_Hunefer_cropped_1.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BD_Hunefer_cropped_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Section of the Book of the Dead for the scribe <a href="/wiki/Hunefer" title="Hunefer">Hunefer</a>, depicting the Weighing of the Heart.</div></div></div>\n<p>Among the most significant and extensively preserved Egyptian writings are <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_funerary_texts" title="Ancient Egyptian funerary texts">funerary texts</a> designed to ensure that deceased souls reached a pleasant afterlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen2000315_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen2000315-57">[57]</a></sup>  The earliest of these are the <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_Texts" title="Pyramid Texts">Pyramid Texts</a>. They are a loose collection of hundreds of spells inscribed on the walls of royal pyramids during the Old Kingdom, intended to magically provide pharaohs with the means to join the company of the gods in the afterlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHornung19991&#8211;5_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHornung19991&#8211;5-58">[58]</a></sup>  The spells appear in differing arrangements and combinations, and few of them appear in all of the pyramids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid200293_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid200293-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the end of the Old Kingdom a new body of funerary spells, which included material from the Pyramid Texts, began appearing in tombs, inscribed primarily on coffins. This collection of writings is known as the <a href="/wiki/Coffin_Texts" title="Coffin Texts">Coffin Texts</a>, and was not reserved for royalty, but appeared in the tombs of non-royal officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2001194&#8211;95_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2001194&#8211;95-60">[60]</a></sup>  In the New Kingdom, several new funerary texts emerged, of which the best-known is the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Dead" title="Book of the Dead">Book of the Dead</a>.  Unlike the earlier books, it often contains extensive illustrations, or vignettes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHornung1999xvii,_14_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHornung1999xvii,_14-61">[61]</a></sup>  The book was copied on papyrus and sold to commoners to be placed in their tombs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuirkeSpencer199298_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuirkeSpencer199298-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Coffin Texts included sections with detailed descriptions of the underworld and instructions on how to overcome its hazards. In the New Kingdom, this material gave rise to several "books of the netherworld", including the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Gates" title="Book of Gates">Book of Gates</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Caverns" title="Book of Caverns">Book of Caverns</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Amduat" title="Amduat">Amduat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen2000316&#8211;17_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen2000316&#8211;17-63">[63]</a></sup>  Unlike the loose collections of spells, these netherworld books are structured depictions of Ra\'s passage through the Duat, and by analogy, the journey of the deceased person\'s soul through the realm of the dead. They were originally restricted to pharaonic tombs, but in the Third Intermediate Period they came to be used more widely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHornung199926&#8211;27,_30_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHornung199926&#8211;27,_30-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practices">Practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philae,_First_Pylon_and_Columnade,_Aswan,_Egypt,_Oct_2004.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="672" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Philae%2C_First_Pylon_and_Columnade%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg/300px-Philae%2C_First_Pylon_and_Columnade%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Philae%2C_First_Pylon_and_Columnade%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg/450px-Philae%2C_First_Pylon_and_Columnade%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Philae%2C_First_Pylon_and_Columnade%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg/600px-Philae%2C_First_Pylon_and_Columnade%2C_Aswan%2C_Egypt%2C_Oct_2004.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philae,_First_Pylon_and_Columnade,_Aswan,_Egypt,_Oct_2004.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First <a href="/wiki/Pylon_(architecture)" title="Pylon (architecture)">pylon</a> and colonnade of the Temple of <a href="/wiki/Isis" title="Isis">Isis</a> at <a href="/wiki/Philae" title="Philae">Philae</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples">Temples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Temples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_temple" title="Egyptian temple">Egyptian temple</a></div>\n<p>Temples existed from the beginning of Egyptian history, and at the height of the civilization they were present in most of its towns. They included both mortuary temples to serve the spirits of deceased pharaohs and temples dedicated to patron gods, although the distinction was blurred because divinity and kingship were so closely intertwined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shafer_2_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Shafer_2-16">[16]</a></sup> The temples were not primarily intended as places for worship by the general populace, and the common people had a complex set of religious practices of their own. Instead, the state-run temples served as houses for the gods, in which physical images which served as their intermediaries were cared for and provided with offerings. This service was believed to be necessary to sustain the gods, so that they could in turn maintain the universe itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_42_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson_42-65">[65]</a></sup> Thus, temples were central to Egyptian society, and vast resources were devoted to their upkeep, including both donations from the monarchy and large estates of their own.  Pharaohs often expanded them as part of their obligation to honor the gods, so that many temples grew to enormous size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson20008&#8211;9,_50_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson20008&#8211;9,_50-66">[66]</a></sup>  However, not all gods had temples dedicated to them, as many gods who were important in official theology received only minimal worship, and many household gods were the focus of popular veneration rather than temple ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200082_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200082-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The earliest Egyptian temples were small, impermanent structures, but through the Old and Middle Kingdoms their designs grew more elaborate, and they were increasingly built out of stone. In the New Kingdom, a basic temple layout emerged, which had evolved from common elements in Old and Middle Kingdom temples. With variations, this plan was used for most of the temples built from then on, and most of those that survive today adhere to it. In this standard plan, the temple was built along a central processional way that led through a series of courts and halls to the sanctuary, which held a statue of the temple\'s god. Access to this most sacred part of the temple was restricted to the pharaoh and the highest-ranking priests. The journey from the temple entrance to the sanctuary was seen as a journey from the human world to the divine realm, a point emphasized by the complex mythological symbolism present in temple architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200572&#8211;82,_86&#8211;89_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200572&#8211;82,_86&#8211;89-68">[68]</a></sup>  Well beyond the temple building proper was the outermost wall. Between the two lay many subsidiary buildings, including workshops and storage areas to supply the temple\'s needs, and the library where the temple\'s sacred writings and mundane records were kept, and which also served as a center of learning on a multitude of subjects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200072&#8211;75_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200072&#8211;75-69">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Theoretically it was the duty of the pharaoh to carry out temple rituals, as he was Egypt\'s official representative to the gods. In reality, ritual duties were almost always carried out by priests. During the Old and Middle Kingdoms, there was no separate class of priests; instead, many government officials served in this capacity for several months out of the year before returning to their secular duties. Only in the New Kingdom did professional priesthood become widespread, although most lower-ranking priests were still part-time. All were still employed by the state, and the pharaoh had final say in their appointments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShafer19979_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShafer19979-70">[70]</a></sup> However, as the wealth of the temples grew, the influence of their priesthoods increased, until it rivaled that of the pharaoh. In the political fragmentation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Intermediate_Period" title="Third Intermediate Period">Third Intermediate Period</a> (c. 1070&#8211;664 BC), the high priests of Amun at Karnak even became the effective rulers of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Egypt" title="Upper Egypt">Upper Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson20009,_25&#8211;26_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson20009,_25&#8211;26-71">[71]</a></sup> The temple staff also included many people other than priests, such as musicians and chanters in temple ceremonies. Outside the temple were artisans and other laborers who helped supply the temple\'s needs, as well as farmers who worked on temple estates. All were paid with portions of the temple\'s income. Large temples were therefore very important centers of economic activity, sometimes employing thousands of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200092&#8211;93_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200092&#8211;93-72">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_rituals_and_festivals">Official rituals and festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Official rituals and festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>State religious practice included both temple rituals involved in the cult of a deity, and ceremonies related to divine kingship. Among the latter were <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_pharaoh" title="Coronation of the pharaoh">coronation ceremonies</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sed_festival" title="Sed festival">sed festival</a>, a ritual renewal of the pharaoh\'s strength that took place periodically during his reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-73">[73]</a></sup> There were numerous temple rituals, including rites that took place across the country and rites limited to single temples or to the temples of a single god. Some were performed daily, while others took place annually or on rarer occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_95_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson_95-74">[74]</a></sup> The most common temple ritual was the morning offering ceremony, performed daily in temples across Egypt. In it, a high-ranking priest, or occasionally the pharaoh, washed, anointed, and elaborately dressed the god\'s statue before presenting it with offerings. Afterward, when the god had consumed the spiritual essence of the offerings, the items themselves were taken to be distributed among the priests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The less frequent temple rituals, or festivals, were still numerous, with dozens occurring every year. These festivals often entailed actions beyond simple offerings to the gods, such as reenactments of particular myths or the symbolic destruction of the forces of disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Most of these events were probably celebrated only by the priests and took place only inside the temple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_95_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson_95-74">[74]</a></sup> However, the most important temple festivals, like the <a href="/wiki/Opet_Festival" title="Opet Festival">Opet Festival</a> celebrated at Karnak, usually involved a procession carrying the god\'s image out of the sanctuary in a model barque to visit other significant sites, such as the temple of a related deity. Commoners gathered to watch the procession and sometimes received portions of the unusually large offerings given to the gods on these occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShafer199727&#8211;28_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShafer199727&#8211;28-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_cults">Animal cults</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Animal cults">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Apis_bull_on_coffin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1061" data-file-width="1063" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Apis_bull_on_coffin.jpg/220px-Apis_bull_on_coffin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Apis_bull_on_coffin.jpg/330px-Apis_bull_on_coffin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Apis_bull_on_coffin.jpg/440px-Apis_bull_on_coffin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apis_bull_on_coffin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Apis bull</div></div></div>\n<p>At many sacred sites, the Egyptians worshipped individual animals which they believed to be manifestations of particular deities. These animals were selected based on specific sacred markings which were believed to indicate their fitness for the role. Some of these cult animals retained their positions for the rest of their lives, as with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apis_(Egyptian_mythology)" title="Apis (Egyptian mythology)">Apis bull</a> worshipped in Memphis as a manifestation of Ptah. Other animals were selected for much shorter periods. These cults grew more popular in later times, and many temples began raising stocks of such animals from which to choose a new divine manifestation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200521,_83_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200521,_83-77">[77]</a></sup> A separate practice developed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twenty-sixth_Dynasty" title="Twenty-sixth Dynasty">Twenty-sixth Dynasty</a>, when people began mummifying any member of a particular animal species as an offering to the god whom the species represented. Millions of mummified <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a>, birds, and other creatures were buried at temples honoring Egyptian deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuirkeSpencer199278,_92&#8211;94_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuirkeSpencer199278,_92&#8211;94-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owen2004_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Owen2004-79">[79]</a></sup> Worshippers paid the priests of a particular deity to obtain and mummify an animal associated with that deity, and the mummy was placed in a cemetery near the god\'s cult center.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oracles">Oracles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Oracles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Egyptians used <a href="/wiki/Oracle" title="Oracle">oracles</a> to ask the gods for knowledge or guidance. Egyptian oracles are known mainly from the New Kingdom and afterward, though they probably appeared much earlier. People of all classes, including the king, asked questions of oracles, and, especially in the late New Kingdom their answers could be used to settle legal disputes or inform royal decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The most common means of consulting an oracle was to pose a question to the divine image while it was being carried in a festival procession, and interpret an answer from the barque\'s movements. Other methods included interpreting the behavior of cult animals, drawing lots, or consulting statues through which a priest apparently spoke. The means of discerning the god\'s will gave great influence to the priests who spoke and interpreted the god\'s message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrankfurter1998145&#8211;152_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrankfurter1998145&#8211;152-81">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_religion">Popular religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Popular religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>While the state cults were meant to preserve the stability of the Egyptian world, lay individuals had their own religious practices that related more directly to daily life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadek19881&#8211;2_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadek19881&#8211;2-82">[82]</a></sup> This popular religion left less evidence than the official cults, and because this evidence was mostly produced by the wealthiest portion of the Egyptian population, it is uncertain to what degree it reflects the practices of the populace as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_46_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson_46-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Popular religious practice included ceremonies marking important transitions in life. These included birth, because of the danger involved in the process, and <a href="/wiki/Naming_ceremony" title="Naming ceremony">naming</a>, because the name was held to be a crucial part of a person\'s identity. The most important of these ceremonies were those surrounding death, because they ensured the soul\'s survival beyond it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche2005128&#8211;131_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche2005128&#8211;131-84">[84]</a></sup> Other religious practices sought to discern the gods\' will or seek their knowledge. These included the interpretation of dreams, which could be seen as messages from the divine realm, and the consultation of oracles. People also sought to affect the gods\' behavior to their own benefit through magical rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Individual Egyptians also prayed to gods and gave them private offerings. Evidence of this type of personal piety is sparse before the New Kingdom. This is probably due to cultural restrictions on depiction of nonroyal religious activity, which relaxed during the Middle and New Kingdoms. Personal piety became still more prominent in the late New Kingdom, when it was believed that the gods intervened directly in individual lives, punishing wrongdoers and saving the pious from disaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ockinga_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ockinga-56">[56]</a></sup> Official temples were important venues for private prayer and offering, even though their central activities were closed to laypeople. Egyptians frequently donated goods to be offered to the temple deity and objects inscribed with prayers to be placed in temple courts. Often they prayed in person before temple statues or in shrines set aside for their use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilkinson_46_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wilkinson_46-83">[83]</a></sup> Yet in addition to temples, the populace also used separate local chapels, smaller but more accessible than the formal temples. These chapels were very numerous and probably staffed by members of the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Households, too, often had their own small shrines for offering to gods or deceased relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESadek198876&#8211;78_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESadek198876&#8211;78-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The deities invoked in these situations differed somewhat from those at the center of state cults. Many of the important popular deities, such as the fertility goddess <a href="/wiki/Taweret" title="Taweret">Taweret</a> and the household protector <a href="/wiki/Bes" title="Bes">Bes</a>, had no temples of their own. However, many other gods, including Amun and Osiris, were very important in both popular and official religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002273,_276&#8211;277_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002273,_276&#8211;277-88">[88]</a></sup> Some individuals might be particularly devoted to a single god. Often they favored deities affiliated with their own region, or with their role in life. The god <a href="/wiki/Ptah" title="Ptah">Ptah</a>, for instance, was particularly important in his cult center of <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a>, but as the patron of craftsmen he received the nationwide veneration of many in that occupation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETraunecker200198_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETraunecker200198-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magic">Magic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Magic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heka_(god)" title="Heka (god)">Heka</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wedjat_(Udjat)_Eye_of_Horus_pendant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="866" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Wedjat_%28Udjat%29_Eye_of_Horus_pendant.jpg/220px-Wedjat_%28Udjat%29_Eye_of_Horus_pendant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Wedjat_%28Udjat%29_Eye_of_Horus_pendant.jpg/330px-Wedjat_%28Udjat%29_Eye_of_Horus_pendant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Wedjat_%28Udjat%29_Eye_of_Horus_pendant.jpg/440px-Wedjat_%28Udjat%29_Eye_of_Horus_pendant.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wedjat_(Udjat)_Eye_of_Horus_pendant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amulet in the shape of the <a href="/wiki/Eye_of_Horus" title="Eye of Horus">Eye of Horus</a>, a common magical symbol</div></div></div>\n<p>The word "magic" could be used to translate the Egyptian term <i>heka</i>, which meant, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_P._Allen" title="James P. Allen">James P. Allen</a> puts it, "the ability to make things happen by indirect means".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen2000156&#8211;157_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen2000156&#8211;157-90">[90]</a></sup> Heka was believed to be a natural phenomenon, the force which was used to create the universe and which the gods employed to work their will. Humans could also use it, however, and magical practices were closely intertwined with religion. In fact, even the regular rituals performed in temples were counted as magic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinch19959&#8211;17_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinch19959&#8211;17-91">[91]</a></sup>  Individuals also frequently employed magical techniques for personal purposes.  Although these ends could be harmful to other people, no form of magic was considered inimical in itself.  Instead, magic was seen primarily as a way for humans to prevent or overcome negative events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Magic was closely associated with the priesthood. Because temple libraries contained numerous magical texts, great magical knowledge was ascribed to the lector priests who studied these texts. These priests often worked outside their temples, hiring out their magical services to laymen. Other professions also commonly employed magic as part of their work, including doctors, scorpion-charmers, and makers of magical amulets.  It is also possible that the peasantry used simple magic for their own purposes, but because this magical knowledge would have been passed down orally, there is limited evidence of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinch199551&#8211;63_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinch199551&#8211;63-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Language was closely linked with heka, to such a degree that <a href="/wiki/Thoth" title="Thoth">Thoth</a>, the god of writing, was sometimes said to be the inventor of heka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinch199516,_28_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinch199516,_28-94">[94]</a></sup>  Therefore, magic frequently involved written or spoken incantations, although these were usually accompanied by ritual actions. Often these rituals invoked the power of an appropriate deity to perform the desired action, using the power of heka to compel it to act. Sometimes this entailed casting the practitioner or subject of a ritual in the role of a character in mythology, thus inducing the god to act toward that person as it had in the myth. Rituals also employed <a href="/wiki/Sympathetic_magic" title="Sympathetic magic">sympathetic magic</a>, using objects believed to have a magically significant resemblance to the subject of the rite. The Egyptians also commonly used objects believed to be imbued with heka of their own, such as the magically protective <a href="/wiki/Amulet" title="Amulet">amulets</a> worn in great numbers by ordinary Egyptians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinch199573&#8211;78_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinch199573&#8211;78-95">[95]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funerary_practices">Funerary practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Funerary practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_funerary_practices" title="Ancient Egyptian funerary practices">Ancient Egyptian funerary practices</a></div>\n<p>Because it was considered necessary for the survival of the soul, preservation of the body was a central part of Egyptian funerary practices. Originally the Egyptians buried their dead in the desert, where the arid conditions <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mummified" title="Mummified">mummified</a> the body naturally. In the Early Dynastic Period, however, they began using tombs for greater protection, and the body was insulated from the <a href="/wiki/Desiccation" title="Desiccation">desiccating</a> effect of the sand and was subject to natural decay. Thus the Egyptians developed their elaborate <a href="/wiki/Embalming" title="Embalming">embalming</a> practices, in which the corpse was artificially desiccated and wrapped to be placed in its coffin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuirkeSpencer199286&#8211;90_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuirkeSpencer199286&#8211;90-96">[96]</a></sup>  The quality of the process varied according to cost, however, and those who could not afford it were still buried in desert graves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002300&#8211;1_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002300&#8211;1-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_the_mouth_ceremony.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1775" data-file-width="2168" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Opening_of_the_mouth_ceremony.jpg/250px-Opening_of_the_mouth_ceremony.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Opening_of_the_mouth_ceremony.jpg/375px-Opening_of_the_mouth_ceremony.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Opening_of_the_mouth_ceremony.jpg/500px-Opening_of_the_mouth_ceremony.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Opening_of_the_mouth_ceremony.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Opening of the Mouth ceremony being performed before the tomb</div></div></div>\n<p>Once the mummification process was complete, the mummy was carried from the deceased person\'s house to the tomb in a funeral procession that included his or her friends and relatives, along with a variety of priests. Before the burial, these priests performed several rituals, including the <a href="/wiki/Opening_of_the_mouth_ceremony" title="Opening of the mouth ceremony">Opening of the mouth ceremony</a> intended to restore the dead person\'s senses and give him or her the ability to receive offerings. Then the mummy was buried and the tomb sealed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2001187&#8211;93_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2001187&#8211;93-98">[98]</a></sup>  Afterward, relatives or hired priests gave food offerings to the deceased in a nearby mortuary chapel at regular intervals.  Over time, families inevitably neglected offerings to long-dead relatives, so most mortuary cults only lasted one or two generations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor200195_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor200195-99">[99]</a></sup>  However, while the cult lasted, the living sometimes wrote letters asking deceased relatives for help, in the belief that the dead could affect the world of the living as the gods did.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002282_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002282-100">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first Egyptian tombs were <a href="/wiki/Mastaba" title="Mastaba">mastabas</a>, rectangular brick structures where kings and nobles were entombed. Each of them contained a subterranean burial chamber and a separate, above ground chapel for mortuary rituals. In the Old Kingdom the mastaba developed into the <a href="/wiki/Pyramid" title="Pyramid">pyramid</a>, which symbolized the primeval mound of Egyptian myth. Pyramids were reserved for royalty, and were accompanied by large mortuary temples sitting at their base. Middle Kingdom pharaohs continued to build pyramids, but the popularity of mastabas waned.  Increasingly, commoners with sufficient means were buried in rock-cut tombs with separate mortuary chapels nearby, an approach which was less vulnerable to tomb robbery.  By the beginning of the New Kingdom even the pharaohs were buried in such tombs, and they continued to be used until the decline of the religion itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2001141&#8211;55_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2001141&#8211;55-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Tombs could contain a great variety of other items, including statues of the deceased to serve as substitutes for the body in case it was damaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian1997100&#8211;1_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian1997100&#8211;1-102">[102]</a></sup>  Because it was believed that the deceased would have to do work in the afterlife, just as in life, burials often included small models of humans to do work in place of the deceased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor200199&#8211;103_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor200199&#8211;103-103">[103]</a></sup>  The tombs of wealthier individuals could also contain furniture, clothing, and other everyday objects intended for use in the afterlife, along with amulets and other items intended to provide magical protection against the hazards of the spirit world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2001107&#8211;10,_200&#8211;13_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2001107&#8211;10,_200&#8211;13-104">[104]</a></sup>  Further protection was provided by funerary texts included in the burial. The tomb walls also bore artwork, such as images of the deceased eating food that were believed to allow him or her to magically receive sustenance even after the mortuary offerings had ceased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQuirkeSpencer199297&#8211;98,_112_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQuirkeSpencer199297&#8211;98,_112-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predynastic_and_Early_Dynastic_periods">Predynastic and Early Dynastic periods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Predynastic and Early Dynastic periods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Narmer-Tjet2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="529" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Narmer-Tjet2.JPG/220px-Narmer-Tjet2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Narmer-Tjet2.JPG/330px-Narmer-Tjet2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Narmer-Tjet2.JPG/440px-Narmer-Tjet2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Narmer-Tjet2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Narmer" title="Narmer">Narmer</a>, a Predynastic ruler, accompanied by men carrying the standards of various local gods</div></div></div>\n<p>The beginnings of Egyptian religion extend into prehistory, though evidence for them comes only from the sparse and ambiguous archaeological record. Careful burials during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predynastic_Egypt" title="Predynastic Egypt">Predynastic period</a> imply that the people of this time believed in some form of an afterlife. At the same time, animals were ritually buried, a practice which may reflect the development of <a href="/wiki/Zoomorphism" title="Zoomorphism">zoomorphic</a> deities like those found in the later religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200312&#8211;15_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200312&#8211;15-106">[106]</a></sup>  The evidence is less clear for gods in human form, and this type of deity may have emerged more slowly than those in animal shape. Each region of Egypt originally had its own patron deity, but it is likely that as these small communities conquered or absorbed each other, the god of the defeated area was either incorporated into the other god\'s mythology or entirely subsumed by it. This resulted in a complex pantheon in which some deities remained only locally important while others developed more universal significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200331_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200331-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid200250&#8211;52_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid200250&#8211;52-108">[108]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Early_Dynastic_Period_(Egypt)" title="Early Dynastic Period (Egypt)">Early Dynastic Period</a> began with the unification of Egypt around 3000&#160;BC. This event transformed Egyptian religion, as some deities rose to national importance and the cult of the divine pharaoh became the central focus of religious activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200315_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200315-109">[109]</a></sup>  Horus was identified with the king, and his cult center in the Upper Egyptian city of <a href="/wiki/Nekhen" title="Nekhen">Nekhen</a> was among the most important religious sites of the period.
