Another important center was <a href="/wiki/Abydos,_Egypt" title="Abydos, Egypt">Abydos</a>, where the early rulers built large funerary complexes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200017&#8211;19_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200017&#8211;19-110">[110]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_and_Middle_Kingdoms">Old and Middle Kingdoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Old and Middle Kingdoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During the Old Kingdom, the priesthoods of the major deities attempted to organize the complicated national pantheon into groups linked by their mythology and worshipped in a single cult center, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ennead" title="Ennead">Ennead</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopolis_(Ancient_Egypt)" title="Heliopolis (Ancient Egypt)">Heliopolis</a> which linked important deities such as <a href="/wiki/Atum" title="Atum">Atum</a>, Ra, Osiris, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Set_(mythology)" title="Set (mythology)">Set</a> in a single creation myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid200251,_81&#8211;85_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid200251,_81&#8211;85-111">[111]</a></sup>  Meanwhile, pyramids, accompanied by large mortuary temple complexes, replaced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mastabas" title="Mastabas">mastabas</a> as the tombs of pharaohs. In contrast with the great size of the pyramid complexes, temples to gods remained comparatively small, suggesting that official religion in this period emphasized the cult of the divine king more than the direct worship of deities. The funerary rituals and architecture of this time greatly influenced the more elaborate temples and rituals used in worshipping the gods in later periods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200578&#8211;79_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunandZivie-Coche200578&#8211;79-112">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pyramide_Djedkare_elevation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="662" data-file-width="1099" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Pyramide_Djedkare_elevation.jpg/220px-Pyramide_Djedkare_elevation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Pyramide_Djedkare_elevation.jpg/330px-Pyramide_Djedkare_elevation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Pyramide_Djedkare_elevation.jpg/440px-Pyramide_Djedkare_elevation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pyramide_Djedkare_elevation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The pyramid complex of <a href="/wiki/Djedkare_Isesi" title="Djedkare Isesi">Djedkare Isesi</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Early in the Old Kingdom, Ra grew in influence, and his cult center at Heliopolis became the nation\'s most important religious site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>  By the Fifth Dynasty, Ra was the most prominent god in Egypt, and had developed the close links with kingship and the afterlife that he retained for the rest of Egyptian history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid200290&#8211;91,_112_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid200290&#8211;91,_112-114">[114]</a></sup>  Around the same time, <a href="/wiki/Osiris" title="Osiris">Osiris</a> became an important afterlife deity. <i>The Pyramid Texts,</i> first written at this time, reflect the prominence of the solar and Osirian concepts of the afterlife, although they also contain remnants of much older traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The texts are an extremely important source for understanding early Egyptian theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid200292_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid200292-116">[116]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 22nd century BC, the Old Kingdom collapsed into the disorder of the First Intermediate Period. Eventually rulers from Thebes reunified the Egyptian nation in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom</a> (c. 2055&#8211;1650 BC). These Theban pharaohs initially promoted their patron god <a href="/wiki/Montu" title="Montu">Montu</a> to national importance, but during the Middle Kingdom, he was eclipsed by the rising popularity of <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002154_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002154-117">[117]</a></sup>  In this new Egyptian state, personal piety grew more important and was expressed more freely in writing, a trend that continued in the New Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shaw_180_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw_180-118">[118]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Kingdom">New Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: New Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Middle Kingdom crumbled in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Intermediate_Period_of_Egypt" title="Second Intermediate Period of Egypt">Second Intermediate Period</a> (c. 1650&#8211;1550 BC), but the country was again reunited by Theban rulers, who became the first pharaohs of the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom</a>. Under the new regime, <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a> became the supreme state god. He was syncretized with Ra, the long-established patron of kingship, and his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precinct_of_Amun-Ra" title="Precinct of Amun-Ra">temple at Karnak</a> in Thebes became Egypt\'s most important religious center.  Amun\'s elevation was partly due to the great importance of Thebes, but it was also due to the increasingly professional priesthood. Their sophisticated theological discussion produced detailed descriptions of Amun\'s universal power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002181&#8211;84,_186_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002181&#8211;84,_186-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann2001166,_191&#8211;92_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann2001166,_191&#8211;92-120">[120]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Increased contact with outside peoples in this period led to the adoption of many Near Eastern deities into the pantheon. At the same time, the subjugated <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubians</a> absorbed Egyptian religious beliefs, and in particular, adopted Amun as their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002276,_304_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002276,_304-121">[121]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_salle_dAkhenaton_(1356-1340_av_J.C.)_(Mus%C3%A9e_du_Caire)_(2076972086).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1464" data-file-width="1165" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/La_salle_dAkhenaton_%281356-1340_av_J.C.%29_%28Mus%C3%A9e_du_Caire%29_%282076972086%29.jpg/220px-La_salle_dAkhenaton_%281356-1340_av_J.C.%29_%28Mus%C3%A9e_du_Caire%29_%282076972086%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/La_salle_dAkhenaton_%281356-1340_av_J.C.%29_%28Mus%C3%A9e_du_Caire%29_%282076972086%29.jpg/330px-La_salle_dAkhenaton_%281356-1340_av_J.C.%29_%28Mus%C3%A9e_du_Caire%29_%282076972086%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/La_salle_dAkhenaton_%281356-1340_av_J.C.%29_%28Mus%C3%A9e_du_Caire%29_%282076972086%29.jpg/440px-La_salle_dAkhenaton_%281356-1340_av_J.C.%29_%28Mus%C3%A9e_du_Caire%29_%282076972086%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_salle_dAkhenaton_(1356-1340_av_J.C.)_(Mus%C3%A9e_du_Caire)_(2076972086).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Akhenaten and his family worshipping the Aten</div></div></div>\n<p>The New Kingdom religious order was disrupted when <a href="/wiki/Akhenaten" title="Akhenaten">Akhenaten</a> acceded, and replaced Amun with the <a href="/wiki/Aten" title="Aten">Aten</a> as the state god. Eventually he eliminated the official worship of most other gods, and moved Egypt\'s capital to a new city at <a href="/wiki/Amarna" title="Amarna">Amarna</a>. This part of Egyptian history, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amarna_period" title="Amarna period">Amarna period</a>, is named after this.  In doing so, Akhenaten claimed unprecedented status: only he could worship the Aten, and the populace directed their worship toward him. The Atenist system lacked well-developed mythology and afterlife beliefs, and the Aten seemed distant and impersonal, so the new order did not appeal to ordinary Egyptians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002215&#8211;18,_238_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002215&#8211;18,_238-122">[122]</a></sup>  Thus, many probably continued to worship the traditional gods in private. Nevertheless, the withdrawal of state support for the other deities severely disrupted Egyptian society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Dijk2000287,_311_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Dijk2000287,_311-123">[123]</a></sup>  Akhenaten\'s successors restored the traditional religious system, and eventually they dismantled all Atenist monuments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002238&#8211;39_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002238&#8211;39-124">[124]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before the Amarna period, popular religion had trended toward more personal relationships between worshippers and their gods. Akhenaten\'s changes had reversed this trend, but once the traditional religion was restored, there was a backlash. The populace began to believe that the gods were much more directly involved in daily life. Amun, the supreme god, was increasingly seen as the final arbiter of human destiny, the true ruler of Egypt. The pharaoh was correspondingly more human and less divine. The importance of oracles as a means of decision-making grew, as did the wealth and influence of the oracles\' interpreters, the priesthood. These trends undermined the traditional structure of society and contributed to the breakdown of the New Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Dijk2000289,_310&#8211;12_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Dijk2000289,_310&#8211;12-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_periods">Later periods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Later periods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Decline_of_ancient_Egyptian_religion" title="Decline of ancient Egyptian religion">Decline of ancient Egyptian religion</a></div>\n<p>In the 1st millennium BC, Egypt was significantly weaker than in earlier times, and in several periods foreigners seized the country and assumed the position of pharaoh. The importance of the pharaoh continued to decline, and the emphasis on popular piety continued to increase. Animal cults, a characteristically Egyptian form of worship, became increasingly popular in this period, possibly as a response to the uncertainty and foreign influence of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002312&#8211;17_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002312&#8211;17-127">[127]</a></sup>  Isis grew more popular as a goddess of protection, magic, and personal salvation, and became the most important goddess in Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200351,_146&#8211;49_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200351,_146&#8211;49-128">[128]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Serapis_Pio-Clementino_Inv689.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="1650" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Serapis_Pio-Clementino_Inv689.jpg/120px-Serapis_Pio-Clementino_Inv689.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Serapis_Pio-Clementino_Inv689.jpg/180px-Serapis_Pio-Clementino_Inv689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Serapis_Pio-Clementino_Inv689.jpg/240px-Serapis_Pio-Clementino_Inv689.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Serapis_Pio-Clementino_Inv689.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Serapis</div></div></div>\n<p>In the 4th century BC, Egypt became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic</a> kingdom under the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_dynasty" title="Ptolemaic dynasty">Ptolemaic dynasty</a> (305&#8211;30 BC), which assumed the pharaonic role, maintaining the traditional religion and building or rebuilding many temples. The kingdom\'s Greek ruling class identified the Egyptian deities with their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeacock2000437&#8211;38_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeacock2000437&#8211;38-129">[129]</a></sup>  From this cross-cultural syncretism emerged <a href="/wiki/Serapis" title="Serapis">Serapis</a>, a god who combined Osiris and Apis with characteristics of Greek deities, and who became very popular among the Greek population. Nevertheless, for the most part the two belief systems remained separate, and the Egyptian deities remained Egyptian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002325&#8211;28_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002325&#8211;28-130">[130]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ptolemaic-era beliefs changed little after Egypt became a <a href="/wiki/Egypt_(Roman_province)" title="Egypt (Roman province)">province</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in 30 BC, with the Ptolemaic kings replaced by distant emperors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeacock2000437&#8211;38_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeacock2000437&#8211;38-129">[129]</a></sup>  The cult of Isis appealed even to Greeks and Romans outside Egypt, and in Hellenized form it spread across the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid2002326_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid2002326-131">[131]</a></sup>  In Egypt itself, as the empire weakened, official temples fell into decay, and without their centralizing influence religious practice became fragmented and localized. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> spread across Egypt, and in the third and fourth centuries AD, edicts by Christian emperors and <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">iconoclasm</a> by local Christians eroded traditional beliefs. While it persisted among the populace for some time, Egyptian religion slowly faded away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrankfurter199823&#8211;30_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrankfurter199823&#8211;30-132">[132]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Th_oltar.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1251" data-file-width="2621" decoding="async" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Th_oltar.JPG/200px-Th_oltar.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Th_oltar.JPG/300px-Th_oltar.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Th_oltar.JPG/400px-Th_oltar.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Th_oltar.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Altar to <a href="/wiki/Thoth" title="Thoth">Thoth</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Kemetism" title="Kemetism">Kemetic</a> follower.</div></div></div>\n<p>Egyptian religion produced the temples and tombs which are ancient Egypt\'s most enduring monuments, but it also influenced other cultures. In pharaonic times many of its symbols, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sphinx" title="Sphinx">sphinx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Winged_sun" title="Winged sun">winged solar disk</a>, were adopted by other cultures across the Mediterranean and Near East, as were some of its deities, such as <a href="/wiki/Bes" title="Bes">Bes</a>.  Some of these connections are difficult to trace.  The Greek concept of <a href="/wiki/Elysium" title="Elysium">Elysium</a> may have derived from the Egyptian vision of the afterlife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAssmann2001392_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAssmann2001392-133">[133]</a></sup>  In late antiquity, the Christian conception of <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">Hell</a> was most likely influenced by some of the imagery of the Duat. Egyptian beliefs also influenced or gave rise to several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Esotericism" title="Esotericism">esoteric</a> belief systems developed by Greeks and Romans, who considered Egypt as a source of mystic wisdom. <a href="/wiki/Hermeticism" title="Hermeticism">Hermeticism</a>, for instance, derived from the tradition of secret magical knowledge associated with <a href="/wiki/Thoth" title="Thoth">Thoth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHornung20011,_9&#8211;11,_73&#8211;75_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHornung20011,_9&#8211;11,_73&#8211;75-134">[134]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times">Modern times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Modern times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Traces of ancient beliefs remained in Egyptian folk traditions into modern times, but its influence on modern societies greatly increased with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Campaign_in_Egypt_and_Syria" title="French Campaign in Egypt and Syria">French Campaign in Egypt and Syria</a> in 1798 and their seeing the monuments and images. As a result of it, Westerners began to study Egyptian beliefs firsthand, and Egyptian religious motifs were adopted into Western art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHornung200175_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHornung200175-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian1997133&#8211;36_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlemingLothian1997133&#8211;36-136">[136]</a></sup>  Egyptian religion has since had a significant <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_influence_in_popular_culture" title="Egyptian influence in popular culture">influence in popular culture</a>. Due to continued interest in Egyptian beliefs, in the late 20th century, several new religious groups going under the blanket term of <a href="/wiki/Kemetism" title="Kemetism">Kemetism</a> have formed based on different reconstructions of ancient Egyptian religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMelton2009841,_847,_851,_855_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMelton2009841,_847,_851,_855-137">[137]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Index_of_Egyptian_mythology_articles" title="Index of Egyptian mythology articles">Index of Egyptian mythology articles</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_religion" title="Prehistoric religion">Prehistoric religion</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Religions_of_the_ancient_Near_East" title="Religions of the ancient Near East">Religions of the ancient Near East</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Traditional_African_religions" title="Traditional African religions">Traditional African religions</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Egyptian_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div pp.&#160;55&#8211;58.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid200253-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid200253_6-0">^</a></b></span> <span id="cite_note-FOOTNOTETeeter2001340&#8211;44-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeeter2001340&#8211;44_8-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFTeeter2001">Teeter 2001</a>, pp.&#160;340&#8211;44.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson200374&#8211;79-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a
